{"Quotes": "\"Zn-modified zeolites are effective catalysts for light alkanes aromatization [14]. The enhanced selectivity of alkane conversion (ethane [57] and propane [8,9]) towards aromatics is attributed to dehydrogenation ability of the loaded zinc species.\"\n\n\"It has been claimed [2,3] that strong Lewis sites, which are generated by zinc, change the mechanism of alkane activation and transformation from the beta scission to a dehydrogenation step.\"\n\n\"For Zn/H-BEA, the kinetic parameters of H/D exchange change dramatically. The rate constants kSiOHAl are at least two orders of magnitude higher. The activation energy of 86-88 kJ mol-1 is rather low. The exchange proceeds with a notable rate at temperatures at least 100 K lower compared to that on H-BEA.\"\n\n\"The promotion may consist of involvement of Zn-alkyl species in the exchange. They can be formed either on ZnO clusters or Zn2+ cations, located on neighbor or remote SiO()Al groups.\"\n\n\"Kazansky et al. [12–14] demonstrated the formation of strong adsorption complexes between small alkanes and Zn2+ cations by IR spectroscopy. They claimed that the activation of the alkane molecules in zeolite occurred on Zn2+ cations.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly Zn-modified zeolites, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations:\n\n1. Composition:\n   - Zinc loading enhances selectivity towards aromatics due to its dehydrogenation ability.\n   - Strong Lewis sites generated by zinc change the mechanism of alkane activation from beta scission to dehydrogenation.\n\n2. Structure:\n   - Zinc can be present as ZnO clusters or Zn2+ cations located on SiO()Al groups in the zeolite framework.\n   - Zn2+ cations form strong adsorption complexes with small alkanes, facilitating their activation.\n\n3. Reaction conditions:\n   - Zn-modified zeolites allow for lower reaction temperatures, at least 100 K lower than unmodified zeolites.\n\n4. Efficiency:\n   - Rate constants for Zn/H-BEA are at least two orders of magnitude higher than for H-BEA.\n   - Activation energy is significantly lower (86-88 kJ/mol for Zn/H-BEA vs. 138 kJ/mol for H-BEA).\n\n5. Reaction mechanism:\n   - Zn-alkyl species are involved as intermediates in the reaction mechanism.\n   - The activation of alkane molecules occurs on Zn2+ cations.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of metal-modified zeolites by allowing for more selective, efficient, and energy-saving processes in alkane transformations such as aromatization, dehydrogenation, and H/D exchange reactions.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.jcat.2007.11.002"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"In general, in propane ODH catalysts made of Voxide supported on silica are more selective than those made with the alumina support, and the latter are more selective than those supported on titania, mainly because of the lower extent of parallel propane combustion, especially at low vanadia loading\"\n\n[2] \"The reduction and catalytic properties of active VOx domains can be significantly affected by the formation of binary dispersed structures. For example, VOx-CrOx and VOx-MoOx mixed structures lead to a higher propane ODH rate and selectivity than VOx domains present at similar surface densities on pure Al2O3 supports\"\n\n[3] \"The activation of the ethane CH bond proceeds through the formation of alkoxides, which decompose to ethylene and a surface OH group or are oxidized to surface-bound oxygenates (acetaldehyde).\"\n\n[4] \"A key factor in the design of efficient catalysts for alkane ODH is the isolation of active sites. Therefore, the isomorphous substitution of active metal species, e.g., vanadium, into microporous and mesoporous materials is an attractive strategy for designing new catalysts for this reaction\"\n\n[5] \"In the case of VCoAPO-18, the presence of both acid sites (related to the presence of Co2+ cations) and redox sites (related to the presence of V5+/4+ and Co3+/2+) seems to be an important factor in achieving high selectivities to ethylene during the ODH of ethane\"\n\n[6] \"The catalytic activity of vanadium oxide catalysts increases with the V2O5 loading, while rates per V-atom reach a maximum on VOx domains of intermediate size, providing a balance between the activity of surface VOx species and their accessibility to reactants.\"\n\n[7] \"For both propane and ethane, reactivity data for catalysts made of supported vanadium oxide are consistent both with kinetically relevant steps involving the dissociation of CH bonds (methylenic C atom in propane) and with a Mars-van Krevelen redox mechanism involving lattice oxygen in CH bond activation.\"\n\n[8] \"One of the best performing catalysts in propane ODH is the VOx-SBA-15 system, prepared by an alcoholic impregnation method, in which the introduction of V species onto the inner walls of SBA-15 provides a system with a large concentration of accessible, isolated and structurally well-defined active sites.\"\n\n[9] \"The selectivity to propylene formation is affected by surface reduction: the higher the surface reduction, the higher the selectivity\"\n\n[10] \"Additives (e.g., P, K, Ca, Ni, Cr, Nb, Mg, Au, Zn and Mo), sometimes added in relevant amount with respect to V, modify acidbasic and redox properties of supported vanadium oxide\"\n\n[11] \"Examples include V-containing high-surface siliceous materials, such as MCM-41, MCM-48 or SBA-15. In these systems, the catalytic activity of vanadium is strongly influenced by its local environment and the co-existence of acid sites in the host material.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) processes:\n\n1. Catalyst composition:\n   - Support material affects selectivity: silica > alumina > titania for propane ODH.\n   - V2O5 loading impacts catalytic activity, with optimal performance at intermediate loadings.\n   - Binary dispersed structures (e.g., VOx-CrOx, VOx-MoOx) can enhance ODH rate and selectivity.\n   - Additives (P, K, Ca, Ni, Cr, Nb, Mg, Au, Zn, Mo) modify acid-base and redox properties.\n\n2. Catalyst structure:\n   - Isolation of active sites is crucial for efficient ODH catalysts.\n   - Isomorphous substitution of active metals into microporous and mesoporous materials (e.g., MCM-41, MCM-48, SBA-15) is an effective design strategy.\n   - VOx-SBA-15 system provides high concentration of accessible, isolated, and well-defined active sites.\n\n3. Reaction mechanism:\n   - Follows Mars-van Krevelen redox mechanism involving lattice oxygen in C-H bond activation.\n   - Ethane ODH proceeds through alkoxide formation, decomposing to ethylene or oxidizing to oxygenates.\n\n4. Surface properties:\n   - Higher surface reduction leads to higher selectivity for propylene formation.\n   - Balance between activity and accessibility of surface VOx species is important.\n\n5. {"Quotes": "[1] \"All catalysts used industrially for the production of maleic anhydride from butane are PVO-based systems.\"\n\n[2] \"The vanadyl pyrophosphate catalyst, due to its particular crystalline structure, shows a singular ability to control its oxidizing power.\"\n\n[3] \"The best selectivity is achieved when desorption of less selective intermediates along the reaction coordinate is avoided because the olefin reactions are less selective on a highly active butane (VO)2P2O7 catalyst.\"\n\n[4] \"The availability of oxygen to the surface is critical in avoiding desorption of less selective olefins. Thus it would appear that major selectivity gains could be achieved if more oxygen could be channeled to the active site.\"\n\n[5] \"A central role in the modification of surface reactivity is adsorption of organic molecules and oxygen species. The mobility of these species under steady-state conditions of reaction is another characteristic of the vanadyl pyrophosphate catalyst in n-butane oxidation\"\n\n[6] \"The vanadium phosphorus oxide system is characterized by facile formation and interconversion of a number of crystalline V(IV) and V(V) phases, and this has led to some confusion in the literature as to the nature of the active phase.\"\n\n[7] \"Kinetics data agree that the hydrocarbon activation is the first step in n-butane oxidation.\"\n\n[8] \"The TAP results suggest that the selectivity of the butane oxidation reaction is influenced by the concentration of these species at the initial butane adsorption site.\"\n\n[9] \"Activation of the alkane hydrocarbon is a further important aspect of this reaction because of its mechanistic relevance in the study of other heterogeneous reactions for functionalization of alkanes\"\n\n[10] \"A very specific surface structure is needed, and it would seem that the binding of intermediates until product is formed (single-site process) is essential to high selectivities.\"\n\n[11] \"For example, the use of an organic solvent during preparation as opposed to an aqueous system results in differing degrees of crystallinity and defect structure of the resulting active phase.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly vanadium phosphorus oxide (VPO) catalysts, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations in processes like oxidative dehydrogenation and n-butane oxidation to maleic anhydride. Key factors include:\n\n1. Catalyst composition: VPO catalysts contain both V(IV) and V(V) phases, with the optimal ratio depending on reaction conditions.\n\n2. Crystal structure: The vanadyl pyrophosphate ((VO)2P2O7) phase is considered the active phase. Its unique crystalline structure allows precise control of oxidizing power, which is crucial for selectivity.\n\n3. Surface properties: The concentration and type of oxygen species on the surface, as well as the adsorption of organic molecules, play a crucial role in determining catalyst activity and selectivity.\n\n4. Reaction conditions: Oxygen availability at the surface is critical. Improving oxygen channeling to active sites could potentially increase selectivity.\n\n5. Preparation method: The use of organic solvents versus aqueous systems during synthesis affects the crystallinity and defect structure of the active phase.\n\n6. Redox properties: The ability of the catalyst to undergo controlled reduction and reoxidation is key to its selectivity.\n\n7. Intermediate binding: A specific surface structure that binds intermediates until product formation (single-site process) is essential for high selectivities.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of VPO catalysts for n-butane oxidation. Their unique ability to selectively activate and transform alkanes without releasing intermediates allows for high selectivity to maleic anhydride, which has not been matched by other catalytic systems.\n\nWhile the focus is on VPO catalysts for butane oxidation, the principles discussed regarding controlled redox properties, oxygen mobility, and surface reactivity are relevant to other metal-based catalysts for alkane transformations. However, specific mechanisms and efficiency factors may differ for other systems such as supported vanadium oxides or metal-exchanged zeolites used in oxidative dehydrogenation or other zeolite-catalyzed reactions.", "Doi": "10.1021/cr00083a003"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Zn- or Ga-modified zeolites effectively catalyze the conversion of light alkanes to aromatics with high selectivity.\"\n\n[2] \"The role of the metal species and acid sites of a zeolite in the enhancement of light alkane conversion to aromatics remains a subject of intense controversy, even after significant emphasis and numerous publications.\"\n\n[3] \"There is agreement on the bifunctional nature of these catalysts, where the metal species are responsible for the dehydrogenation of alkanes, while the Brnsted acid sites are involved in the oligomerization and cyclization of olefins.\"\n\n[4] \"When zeolites are modified with Zn, the picture of hydrogen exchange changes. Regioselectivity of the exchange is observed on Zn/H-ZSM-5 and Zn/H-BEA for propane and n-butane.\"\n\n[5] \"A significant increase in the reaction rates with lowering apparent activation energies demonstrate the promoting effect of Zn on the reaction of H/D hydrogen exchange for C1n-C4 alkanes.\"\n\n[6] \"The acceleration of H/D exchange, coupled with the decreased activation energy for Zn-modified zeolites, demonstrates that the CH bonds in alkanes break and form more easily and reversibly with intermediate involvement of Zn species.\"\n\n[7] \"The strong influence of Zn on the activation of CH bonds of small alkanes by the zeolite Brnsted sites is achieved by involvement in the exchange of Znalkyl species, whose formation from alkanes precedes its further involvement in interaction with Brnsted acid sites.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly metal-exchanged zeolites, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations. Bifunctional nature: These catalysts have metal species responsible for alkane dehydrogenation and Brønsted acid sites involved in olefin oligomerization and cyclization.\n\n3. Regioselectivity: Zn modification introduces regioselectivity in hydrogen exchange for propane and n-butane on Zn/H-ZSM-5 and Zn/H-BEA zeolites.\n\n4. Reaction kinetics: Zn promotes H/D hydrogen exchange for C1-C4 alkanes, increasing reaction rates and lowering activation energies.\n\n5. C-H bond activation: Zn-alkyl species formation facilitates easier breaking and formation of C-H bonds, interacting with Brønsted acid sites.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of metal-modified zeolites by improving catalytic performance in alkane transformation processes. {"Quotes": "[1] \"The dehydroaromatization of methane to benzene has been investigated over a 2 wt% Mo/ZSM-5 catalyst in the absence of an added oxidant.\"\n\n[2] \"Under typical CH4 reaction conditions at 700C, 60–80% of the original Mo6+ ions are reduced to form Mo2C, with the remaining Mo occurring primarily as Mo4+ and traces of Mo5+ ions.\"\n\n[3] \"Exposure of a 2 wt% Mo/ZSM-5 catalyst to CH4 or to CH4/H2 at 700C causes reduction of Mo ions to Mo2C, rather than to metallic Mo.\"\n\n[4] \"Since preformation of Mo2C on the catalyst, without coke deposition, does not completely eliminate the induction period, it is believed that the clean surface of Mo2C may be too reactive to form higher hydrocarbons and, thus, that a coke-modified Mo2C surface may be the actual active species in the formation of ethylene as the reaction intermediate.\"\n\n[5] \"The presence of a certain concentration of acid sites in Mo/ZSM-5 catalysts is thus necessary to achieve a high benzene selectivity and a high methane conversion, since the formation of aromatics is thermodynamically favored\"\n\n[6] \"During preparation and/or pretreatment of the catalyst, a portion of the Mo ions diffuses into the channels of the zeolite. The amount of Mo ions within the channels depends on the temperature, time, and atmosphere of calcination.\"\n\n[7] \"It appears that the dispersion of molybdenum oxide species on the surface of ZSM-5 may occur slowly and/or may be restricted to a short distance.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly Mo/ZSM-5, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, especially in the dehydroaromatization of methane to benzene. Catalyst structure: Mo species are initially present on the external surface of ZSM-5 and partially diffuse into the zeolite channels during preparation and pretreatment. The distribution of Mo affects catalyst performance.\n\n3. Reaction conditions: High temperature (700°C) and the absence of added oxidants are critical. These conditions promote the reduction of Mo6+ to Mo2C, which is the active species for methane activation.\n\n4. The formation of Mo2C corresponds to the initial activation period for benzene production.\n\n5. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Supported vanadium oxide in heterogeneous catalysis: elucidating the structureactivity relationship with spectroscopy\"\n\n[2] \"Reactivity in the confined spaces of zeolites: the interplay between spectroscopy and theory to develop structureactivity relationships for catalysis\"\n\n[3] \"Binuclear sites are argued to account for the enhanced activity of oxygenated gallium-exchanged high-silica zeolites in alkane dehydrogenation.\"\n\n[4] \"These sites, however, tend to decompose via water desorption upon the catalytic reaction resulting in less reactive reduced Ga+ ions.\"\n\n[5] \"As per predictions from the quantum-chemical calculations, the experimental results show that the high alkane dehydrogenation activity can be maintained by in situ hydrolysis of the reduced extraframework Ga species.\"\n\n[6] \"Clearly, co-feeding of water leads to a substantial increase of the propane conversion. The conversion passes a maximum at pH2O of 0.3 kPa.\"\n\n[7] \"The promoting effect of water is due to the formation of reactive partly hydrolyzed gallium species such as [HGaOGaOH]2+ and [HGa(OH)2GaH]2+ discussed above.\"\n\n[8] \"Addition of water to the hydrocarbon feed increases significantly the dehydrogenation activity. The main product remains propylene although with increasing water content, formation of the products of propane cracking (methane and ethylene) is observed.\"\n\n[9] \"Formation of less stable intermediate products in the catalytic cycle facilitates the regeneration of the active site. Indeed, moderate barriers are calculated for H2 recombination in the catalytic cycle involving [HGa(OH)(O)Ga]2+ species.\"\n\n[10] \"Although earlier isolated mononuclear GaO+ have been proposed as the active sites in Ga-containing high-silica zeolites, the present results indicate that such species should oligomerize in the microporous space, independent of the Al distribution.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in processes such as oxidative dehydrogenation and zeolite-catalyzed reactions:\n\n1. Structure-activity relationships: For supported vanadium oxides and zeolites, understanding the structure-activity relationship is crucial for elucidating catalytic behavior in heterogeneous catalysis.\n\n2. Confined spaces in zeolites: The reactivity in zeolites is greatly affected by their confined spaces, with spectroscopy and theory interplaying to develop structure-activity relationships.\n\n3. Binuclear sites: In gallium-exchanged high-silica zeolites, binuclear sites are believed to enhance activity in alkane dehydrogenation, rather than isolated mononuclear GaO+ species.\n\n4. Water's role: The presence of water significantly affects catalyst performance. Co-feeding water increases dehydrogenation activity by forming reactive partly hydrolyzed gallium species. An optimal water partial pressure (0.3 kPa) was found for maximum conversion.\n\n5. Active site regeneration: Less stable intermediate products in the catalytic cycle facilitate the regeneration of active sites, with moderate barriers calculated for H2 recombination.\n\n6. Species oligomerization: Gallium species tend to oligomerize in the microporous space of zeolites, independent of aluminum distribution, leading to more active catalytic sites.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of these catalysts by:\n- Allowing for fine-tuning of catalyst performance through control of metal species structure and composition.\n- Enabling the maintenance of high catalytic activity through careful control of reaction conditions, such as water co-feeding.\n- Providing insights into catalyst deactivation mechanisms and ways to mitigate them, ensuring longer catalyst lifetimes and more efficient processes.\n- Optimizing desired product yields while minimizing unwanted side reactions.", "Doi": "10.1039/b815943b"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Supported vanadium oxide catalysts, containing surface vanadia species on oxide supports, are extensively employed as catalysts for many hydrocarbon oxidation reactions.\"\n\n[2] \"The molecular structures of these surface vanadia species are tentatively thought to consist of a terminal V=O bond and three bridging V-O-support bonds for the isolated species, and a terminal V=O bond with one bridging V-O-support and two bridging V-O-V bonds for the polymerized species.\"\n\n[3] \"The bridging V-O-support bond appears to be associated with the critical oxygen required for hydrocarbon oxidation reactions since changing the specific oxide support dramatically affects the TOF: approximately four orders of magnitude for methanol oxidation to formaldehyde and two-orders of magnitude for butane oxidation to maleic anhydride.\"\n\n[4] \"The general trend appears to be CeOz>ZrO2 TiO2>NbzOs>A12Os>SiO2, which inversely correlates with the Sanderson electronegativity of the oxide support cations.\"\n\n[5] \"As a result of these physical and chemical characteristics, supported vanadia catalysts tend to be among the most active and selective supported metal oxide catalysts for hydrocarbon oxidation reactions.\"\n\n[6] \"The oxidative dehydrogenation of propane to propylene over V2O5/Nb2O5 was not dependent on the concentration of the bridging V-O-V bonds\"\n\n[7] \"Oxidation reactions requiring only one surface vanadia sites are generally not sensitive to the surface vanadia coverage and the presence of noninteracting metal oxide additives. Oxidation reactions requiring multiple surface vanadia sites are very sensitive to surface vanadia coverage and the presence of noninteracting metal oxide additives.\"\n\n[8] \"The addition of surface niobia is especially informative since it has a minimal effect on the surface vanadia structure, see Section 5.6 above, and its presence primarily increases the number of adjacent surface metal oxide sites for the surface vanadia species.\"\n\n[9] \"Interacting additives affect all hydrocarbon oxidation reactions since they directly alter the structure and reactivity of the surface vanadia sites.\"\n\n[10] \"Acidic and basic additives also influence the selectivity of hydrocarbon oxidation reactions, but the effect appears to be reaction specific and cannot be generalized with the limited data currently available.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of supported vanadium oxide catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in oxidative dehydrogenation processes. Key factors contributing to their industrial significance include:\n\n1. Support material: The choice of oxide support (e.g., CeO2, ZrO2, TiO2, Nb2O5, Al2O3, SiO2) dramatically affects catalyst performance. The bridging V-O-support bond is critical, as it can change the turnover frequency (TOF) for hydrocarbon oxidation reactions by up to four orders of magnitude.\n\n2. Surface structure: Surface vanadia species typically consist of isolated species with a terminal V=O bond and three bridging V-O-support bonds, or polymerized species with a terminal V=O bond, one V-O-support bond, and two V-O-V bonds.\n\n3. Support electronegativity: The reactivity trend (CeO2 > ZrO2 > TiO2 > Nb2O5 > Al2O3 > SiO2) inversely correlates with the Sanderson electronegativity of the oxide support cations, affecting the basicity of the bridging oxygens.\n\n4. Surface coverage and additives: The sensitivity to surface vanadia coverage and non-interacting metal oxide additives depends on whether the reaction requires single or multiple surface vanadia sites. For example, the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane to propylene over V2O5/Nb2O5 was not dependent on the concentration of bridging V-O-V bonds.\n\n5. Interacting additives: These directly alter the structure and reactivity of surface vanadia sites, affecting all hydrocarbon oxidation reactions.\n\n6. Acidic and basic additives: These influence the selectivity of hydrocarbon oxidation reactions, though their effects are reaction-specific and not yet fully understood.\n\nThese factors allow for the tuning of catalyst properties to optimize performance in specific alkane transformation processes, contributing to the industrial significance of supported vanadium oxide catalysts. Their unique characteristics make them among the most active and selective supported metal oxide catalysts for hydrocarbon oxidation reactions.", "Doi": "10.1016/s0926-860x(97)00021-5"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Ga/H-ZSM-5 catalysts are most active and selective for the aromatisation of propane [1]. It is widely accepted that their activity and selectivity result from their bifunctional properties, whereby gallium species are responsible for their dehydrogenation-hydrogenation activity and the Brönsted acid sites of the zeolite provide the centres necessary for chain growth.\"\n\n[2] \"Recently, it was shown that dual active sites are responsible for the activation of propane on Ga/H-ZSM-5 [2-4]. These dual active sites consist of highly dispersed (Ga3+, O2-) ions pairs, acting in synergy with neighbouring Brönsted sites which initially activate propane as a protonated pseudo-cyclopropane species.\"\n\n[3] \"Changes in the propane aromatisation activity and selectivity of these catalysts as a function of the number of reaction (reducing conditions)-regeneration (oxidative conditions) cycles sustained were explained by the migration of gallium species in and out of the zeolite intracrystalline void space [5] as substantiated by detailed catalyst characterisation [6].\"\n\n[4] \"Hydrogen treatment increases activity and selectivity to aromatics while decreasing the formation of methane. Subsequent oxygen treatment enhances further the activity and aromatics selectivity and has little effect on methane selectivity.\"\n\n[5] \"These improvements of catalytic performance are explained by gallium migration in reducing conditions. The concentration of highly dispersed gallium species is thereby enhanced. These species, in association with Brønsted centers, were reported previously to constitute the dual catalytic sites responsible for alkane activation.\"\n\n[6] \"Several authors have also reported the inhibitory effect of hydrogen on propane transformation over Zn/H-ZSM-5 [24] and Ga/H-ZSM-5 catalysts [25]. A possible explanation is that the limiting step of the propane dehydrogenation is the removal of hydrogen [26].\"\n\n[7] \"H2-O2 pretreatment thus appear to be a promising way to preactivate and stabilise Ga-modified aromatisation catalysts in commercial operation.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly Ga/H-ZSM-5, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, especially in processes such as propane aromatization. Key factors include:\n\n1. Composition: The bifunctional nature of Ga/H-ZSM-5 catalysts, with gallium species responsible for dehydrogenation-hydrogenation activity and Brönsted acid sites from the zeolite enabling chain growth, is crucial for their high activity and selectivity.\n\n2. Structure: Dual active sites consisting of highly dispersed (Ga3+, O2-) ion pairs working synergistically with neighboring Brönsted sites are responsible for propane activation. The dispersion of gallium species within the zeolite structure is vital for catalyst performance.\n\n3. Reaction conditions:\n   a) Reducing conditions (H2 treatment) increase gallium dispersion, leading to higher activity and aromatics selectivity while reducing methane formation.\n   b) Oxidative conditions (O2 treatment) following H2 treatment further boost activity and aromatics selectivity without affecting methane selectivity.\n   c) Hydrogen can have an inhibitory effect on propane transformation, possibly due to its impact on the rate-limiting step of propane dehydrogenation.\n\n4. Catalyst activation: H2-O2 pretreatment cycles dramatically improve catalytic performance by optimizing gallium dispersion and oxidation state. This pretreatment eliminates the induction period and could be industrially significant for preactivating and stabilizing catalysts before use in aromatization units.\n\n5. Reaction-regeneration cycles: The migration of gallium species in and out of the zeolite intracrystalline void space during reaction (reducing) and regeneration (oxidative) cycles affects catalyst performance over time.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of these catalysts by enhancing their activity, selectivity, and stability in alkane transformation processes, particularly in propane aromatization. The ability to fine-tune catalyst properties through composition, structure, and pretreatment conditions allows for optimized performance in industrial-scale applications.", "Doi": "10.1016/s0920-5861(96)00015-6"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Previous studies have shown that supported vanadia is the most active and selective simple metal oxide for alkane ODH, because its reducible nature leads to the rapid redox cycles required for a catalytic turnover (15).\"\n\n[2] \"UVvisible spectroscopy suggests that V5+ is present in distorted tetrahedrally coordinated species at low vanadia surface densities and that some of these tetrahedrally coordinated cations remain at higher surface densities, coexisting with an increasing fraction of V5+ present in distorted tetragonal pyramidal coordination, similar to that present in V2O5 (25).\"\n\n[3] \"For both ethane and propane reactants, these apparent turnover rates reach a maximum at intermediate vanadia surface densities. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Isolated molybdate species supported on silica are reported to have the highest specific activity and selectivity for the direct oxidation of methane to formaldehyde.\"\n\n[2] \"On the basis of evidence from extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) and Raman spectroscopy, the Mo atoms in this catalyst are present as isolated, pentacoordinated molybdate species containing a single MoO bond.\"\n\n[3] \"It is proposed that peroxides are produced by the reaction of O2 with a small concentration of reduced molybdate species and that the reaction of CH4 with these peroxide species leads to the formation of formaldehyde.\"\n\n[4] \"Low concentrations of H2O were observed to enhance the activity of MoOx/SiO2 for CH4 oxidation to formaldehyde.\"\n\n[5] \"During CH4 oxidation to formaldehyde, the catalyst undergoes only a very small degree of reduction and typically only 50500 ppm of MoVI is reduced to MoIV.\"\n\n[6] \"It was established that treatment of MoOx/SiO2 at 873 K under pure methane reduces the dispersed molybdate species to only a limited extent and results mainly in the deposition of amorphous carbon.\"", "English": "The literature focuses on molybdenum oxide catalysts supported on silica (MoOx/SiO2) for methane oxidation to formaldehyde, rather than vanadium oxides or zeolites specifically. However, several insights can be drawn regarding the influence of composition, structure, and reaction conditions on the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations:\n\n1. Composition and structure: Isolated, pentacoordinated molybdate species containing a single Mo=O bond and supported on silica show the highest activity and selectivity for methane oxidation to formaldehyde. The isolated nature of these species contributes to their high specific activity.\n\n2. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Catalytic reactions of hydrocarbons in zeolites can be profoundly affected by extraframework transition-metal cationic clusters. Structure: The ZSM-5 framework provides specific sites for metal ion stabilization, such as the a-site (a six-membered ring composed of two five-membered rings on the straight channel walls), which is important for Cu+ localization.\n\n3. Reaction conditions: High-temperature treatments are used to form copper adsorption centers, either through reduction of dimeric oxygen-bridged species or direct reaction with CuCl vapor.\n\n4. Mechanism: Ethane adsorption on Cu+ sites occurs in different geometries (Z1 and Z2). The bonding involves a synergetic combination of ligand-to-metal electron donation and metal-to-ligand back-donation, forming stable σ-type donor-acceptor complexes.\n\n5. Efficiency: The formation of these complexes results in significant perturbation of the adsorbed ethane molecules, leading to activation without dissociation.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of CuZSM-5 catalysts by enabling strong, selective interactions with alkanes under controlled conditions. This allows for potential applications in various processes, including selective NO decomposition and alkane transformations. The ability to form stable, reversible adsorption complexes with alkanes at room temperature is particularly noteworthy for potential separation or catalytic processes.", "Doi": "10.1039/b418498j"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Unpromoted HZSM-5 is, however, a poor dehydrogenation catalyst; addition of promoters is required for such application.\"\n\n[2] \"Modification of the zeolite with zinc or gallium has been successful for this purpose.\"\n\n[3] \"For the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane and n-butane, it has been reported that the alkene yield is highest over Fe3+ silicalite, and rapid cracking and combustion of propane occurred over ion-exchanged Fe/ZSM-5 and FeOx/silicalite.\"\n\n[4] \"Upon condensation of the vapor of a volatile precursor on the H-form of the zeolite, the concentration of Brnsted sites is lowered drastically and a high level of ion exchange is obtained. Subsequent treatment with H2O to remove chlorine from the precursor induces hydrolysis of the metal ions.\"\n\n[5] \"Hydrolysis converts naked ions into oxo ions, such as (GaO)+ and (HOFeOFeOH)2+. They are identified by temperature-programmed reduction with H2 and CO and, in the case of the binuclear Fe3+ ion, by ESR spectroscopy.\"\n\n[6] \"The Ga2O3 clusters are easily reduced to Ga+ at 450 C. With (HOFeOFeOH)2+ in cationic exchange positions reduction is reversible at 500 C.\"\n\n[7] \"In the case of Zn/ZSM-5, isolated Zn2+ cations compensate the charge of two Al-centered tetrahedra or attach themselves to one silanol group or ZnOH and one Al-centered tetrahedron. In this state, the Zn ions are stabilized and cannot be reduced below 800 C.\"\n\n[8] \"When Ga, Zn, and Fe replace protons in HZSM-5, they form strong Lewis acid sites.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly supported vanadium oxides and metal-exchanged zeolites, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations in processes such as oxidative dehydrogenation and zeolite-catalyzed reactions.\n\nFor zeolite-catalyzed reactions, the addition of promoters like zinc or gallium to HZSM-5 improves its dehydrogenation catalytic activity. The method of introducing these metals, such as sublimation of volatile compounds followed by hydrolysis, affects the nature of the active sites. This process reduces Brønsted acid sites and creates strong Lewis acid sites, which can alter the catalyst's activity and selectivity.\n\nThe nature of the metal and its oxidation state play crucial roles in oxidative dehydrogenation. For instance, Fe3+ in silicalite shows high alkene yield for propane and n-butane dehydrogenation, while Fe/ZSM-5 promotes cracking and combustion. The structure of the metal species, such as isolated ions, oxo-species, or small clusters, influences the reaction pathway and product distribution.\n\nThe preparation method affects the final structure of the metal species in the zeolite. Hydrolysis can create oxo-ions like (GaO)+ and (HOFeOFeOH)2+, which have distinct catalytic properties. The reducibility of these species varies, with Ga2O3 clusters easily reduced at 450°C, (HOFeOFeOH)2+ undergoing reversible reduction at 500°C, and Zn2+ ions stable up to 800°C.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of metal-exchanged zeolites in alkane transformations. The ability to tune the catalyst's properties through metal selection, preparation method, and post-treatment allows for optimization of activity, selectivity, and stability for specific industrial processes. The combination of strong Lewis acidity from the metal species and the shape-selective properties of the zeolite framework makes these materials particularly attractive for industrial applications in petrochemical processes and fine chemical synthesis.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp990116l"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Iron complexes in the ZSM-5 zeolite matrix (α-centers) are shown to perform single-turnover cycles of methane oxidation to methanol at room temperature when nitrous oxide is used as a source of oxygen.\"\n\n[2] \"FeZSM-5 zeolite containing iron complexes that form under thermal activation (called α-centers [7]) is able to perform single-turnover cycles of selective methane oxidation to methanol at room temperature when nitrous oxide (N2O) is the source of oxygen.\"\n\n[3] \"At temperatures lower than 573 K, N2O decomposition on α-centers quantitatively follows reaction (1): N2O + ( )α = (O)α + N2, loading the active centers with α-oxygen [7].\"\n\n[4] \"There are α-centers whose activity gives FeZSM-5 zeolites a unique ability to catalyze direct oxidation of benzene to phenol with nitrous oxide at 573-623 K, providing nearly 100 % selectivity [9,14].\"\n\n[5] \"Binuclear iron centers are a particular feature of MMO compared to the mononuclear centers of other monooxygenases [1]. There is some indirect evidence favoring the binuclear structure of the active iron complexes in the ZSM-5 matrix\"", "English": "The literature discusses how the composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, specifically iron-exchanged ZSM-5 zeolites (FeZSM-5), influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations. Key points include:\n\n1. Composition: Iron complexes (α-centers) in the ZSM-5 zeolite matrix are the active sites.\n\n2. Structure: Evidence suggests the α-centers may be binuclear iron complexes, similar to the active sites in methane monooxygenase enzymes.\n\n3. Reaction conditions:\n   - Room temperature operation for methane oxidation to methanol\n   - Use of nitrous oxide (N2O) as the oxygen source\n   - Temperatures below 573 K for N2O decomposition and α-oxygen loading\n   - Higher temperatures (573-623 K) for steady-state oxidation of benzene to phenol\n\n4. Mechanism: Single-turnover cycles for methane oxidation; steady-state catalysis for benzene oxidation. N2O decomposition on α-centers loads them with active oxygen, which then reacts with the alkane substrate.\n\n5. Efficiency and Selectivity: High selectivity is observed, with methanol as the only product in methane oxidation, and nearly 100% selectivity in benzene to phenol conversion.\n\n6. Industrial significance: The ability to selectively oxidize methane at room temperature and benzene at moderate temperatures with high selectivity is potentially valuable industrially. The use of N2O as an oxygen source and the ability to operate at relatively low temperatures contribute to their potential importance.\n\nThese factors collectively contribute to the unique properties of FeZSM-5 catalysts, including low-temperature operation, high selectivity, and versatility in oxidizing different substrates, which enhance their potential industrial significance in alkane transformations.", "Doi": "10.1007/bf02478380"},

Key factors contributing to their industrial significance include:\n\n1. Catalyst composition: The type of metal (e.g., Mo, W, V) and its oxidation state (e.g., Mo6+) affect catalytic performance.\n\n2. {"Quotes": "[1] \"ZnMFI catalyzes dehydrogenation and aromatization of light paraffins, etc.\"\n\n[2] \"Unlike ion exchange or incipient wetness impregnation, the reaction of hydrogen forms of MFI with zinc vapour at 773 K results in the practically complete substitution of protons by zinc ions.\"\n\n[3] \"The catalytic properties of high-silica zeolites modified with bivalent cations are quite different from those of faujasites containing the same cations.\"\n\n[4] \"Zinc or other bivalent ions with only partially compensated positive charges exhibit an unusually high chemical activity. This explains the specific catalytic and adsorption properties of high-silica zeolites modified with the bivalent transition metal cations.\"\n\n[5] \"Our recent results also demonstrated that MFI modified with bivalent Zn or Co cations possess unusual adsorption properties. {"Quotes": "[1] \"The oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of propane over a supported vanadia catalyst is of great interest because of its importance in industrial catalysis.\"\n\n[2] \"Experimental studies of catalyst structure (25) generally agree that when the concentration of vanadium oxide species is low, isolated VO4 units form on the support surface. These units are assumed to contain a vertical VO double bond and three bridging VOS linkages, where S represents the support metal cation (S = Al, Si, Ti, etc.).\"\n\n[3] \"The dependence of the reactivity on coverage of the support surface by vanadium oxide is unclear in the literature.\"\n\n[4] \"Experimental studies of the ODH reaction with supported vanadia catalysts have generally supported the reaction mechanism originally proposed by Mars and van Krevelen.\"\n\n[5] \"The experimental barriers for propane ODH on supported vanadium oxide are in the range 2030 kcal/mol, (4,10,22) which are much lower than the value of about 80 kcal/mol found in this study for the water elimination step.\"\n\n[6] \"Our results point toward a possible reason as to why the interaction of vanadium oxide with a support may lower the barrier for the initial dissociative adsorption of propane and propene elimination steps in the ODH reaction pathways investigated here\"\n\n[7] \"We have carried out a hybrid density functional study of mechanisms for oxidative dehydrogenation of propane on the (010) surface of V2O5. The surface was modeled using both vanadium oxide clusters and a periodic slab.\"\n\n[8] \"The potential energy surface is found to have large barriers, which are lowered somewhat when the possibility of a triplet state is considered.\"\n\n[9] \"The barriers on the reaction pathway are 4580 kcal/mol above the energy of the reactants, which are much larger than found experimentally for propane ODH on supported vanadia.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly supported vanadium oxides, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, especially in oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) processes.\n\n1. Composition and Structure:\n   - At low concentrations, vanadium oxide forms isolated VO4 units on the support surface, containing a vertical VO double bond and three bridging VOS linkages.\n   - The concentration of vanadium oxide species affects the catalyst structure, but the relationship between reactivity and surface coverage is not fully understood.\n   - The interaction between vanadium oxide and the support material plays a crucial role in determining catalytic activity.\n\n2. Reaction Mechanism:\n   - Supported vanadia catalysts generally follow the Mars-van Krevelen mechanism for ODH reactions.\n   - The potential energy surface of the reaction has large barriers, which can be somewhat lowered when considering the possibility of a triplet state.\n\n3. Efficiency and Barriers:\n   - Experimental barriers for propane ODH on supported vanadium oxide are much lower (20-30 kcal/mol) compared to theoretical calculations on clean V2O5 surfaces (about 80 kcal/mol).\n   - The interaction of vanadium oxide with a support may lower barriers for initial dissociative adsorption of propane and propene elimination steps.\n   - Theoretical calculations show barriers of 45-80 kcal/mol for the reaction pathway, which are still higher than experimental values for supported vanadia.\n\n4. Industrial Significance:\n   - The lower activation barriers and enhanced catalytic activity of supported vanadium oxide catalysts contribute to their industrial importance in alkane transformation processes.\n   - The ability to tune catalyst performance through composition and structure offers opportunities for optimizing industrial processes.\n\n5. Modeling and Research:\n   - Hybrid density functional studies have been conducted to model the ODH of propane on V2O5 surfaces using both vanadium oxide clusters and periodic slabs.\n   - The discrepancy between theoretical and experimental results highlights the need for further research to fully understand the mechanisms behind the enhanced catalytic activity of supported vanadium oxide catalysts.\n\nWhile the provided information does not specifically address metal-exchanged zeolites, the principles of how support materials influence catalyst performance may be applicable to zeolite-based systems as well.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp056228w"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"It has been found that, when propane is used as the reactant at 5OOC and 0.1 MPa, the BTX selectivity can be increased from ca. 10% (H-ZSM-5) to ca. 40 to 45% after treatment of the base zeolite with solutions of zinc or gallium nitrate, such that the final zinc or gallium content is ca. 1.0 mass-%.\"\n\n[2] \"On Zn-ZSM-5 part of the hydrogen atom pool lost on aromatization is released as dihydrogen and an improved BTX selectivity results.\"\n\n[3] \"It appears that Ga-ZSM-5 or GaZn-ZSM-5 catalysts operate in a similar manner, but gallium-containing samples are preferred since zinc volatilization occurs at the reaction temperatures used for samples containing zinc alone.\"\n\n[4] \"The contact time has been shown to have a strong influence on the product distribution obtained in these systems. To summarize, the observed selectivity to aromatics depends on the form of the zeolite used and on the contact time.\"\n\n[5] \"A comparative study of the conversion of propane, n-butane and i-butane over Zn-ZSM-5 reveals that the alkanes differ in terms of activity and BTX selectivity. i-Butane is the most reactive of the three alkanes and also yields the highest percentage hydrogen recovery as dihydrogen. However, it displays the lowest BTX selectivity.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in zeolite-catalyzed reactions such as the conversion of light alkanes to aromatics (BTX).\n\nComposition: Adding metals like zinc or gallium (around 1.0 mass-%) to H-ZSM-5 zeolites increases BTX selectivity from about 10% to 40-45% for propane conversion at 500°C and 0.1 MPa. Gallium-containing catalysts are preferred over zinc-containing ones due to zinc volatilization at high temperatures.\n\nStructure: ZSM-5 type zeolites are effective for this transformation. The metal component (Zn or Ga) is introduced by treating H-ZSM-5 with aqueous solutions of the respective metal nitrate.\n\nReaction conditions: Temperature (typically 500°C) and pressure (0.1 MPa) affect the process. Contact time strongly influences product distribution and aromatics selectivity.\n\nMechanism: On Zn-ZSM-5 and Ga-ZSM-5, part of the hydrogen lost during aromatization is released as dihydrogen, improving BTX selectivity. Different alkanes (propane, n-butane, i-butane) show varying activities and BTX selectivities.\n\nIndustrial significance: These catalysts allow for tunable selectivity and improved yields of desired aromatic products from light alkanes, which is important for upgrading lower-value hydrocarbons. The ability to convert light alkanes to more valuable aromatic compounds (BTX) is industrially important, making these processes more economically viable for industrial applications.", "Doi": "10.1016/s0166-9834(00)80384-x"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Incorporation of zinc cations into high-silica zeolites enhances the selectivity of zeolite catalysts towards light alkane dehydrogenation and aromatization [13].\"\n\n[2] \"It seems that the incorporated Zn2+ cations act as strong Lewis sites and change the mechanism of the alkane activation and transformation favoring a hydrogen removal step [2, 410].\"\n\n[3] \"Analysis of the results on characterization of zinc species suggested that Zn/H-MFI involves three types of Zn species: small and large ZnO clusters and zinc cations.\"\n\n[4] \"For Zn/H-MFI sample used in this study with high Zn content, (OZ)(ZnOZn)2+(OZ) or (ZO)Zn+OSi cations should preferably form based on the literature data [2, 22]. In Zn/H-MFI catalysts, three types of zinc species are present: small and large ZnO clusters, and zinc cations. The specific form of zinc species depends on the zinc content, with high zinc content favoring the formation of (OZ)(ZnOZn)2+(OZ) or (ZO)Zn+OSi cations, although Zn2+ or ZnOH+ may also be present.\n\nThese zinc species, especially Zn2+ cations, act as strong Lewis sites and change the mechanism of alkane activation and transformation, favoring hydrogen removal. The structure of the catalyst plays a crucial role in the reaction mechanism. Zn2+ cations located at neighboring or distantly separated basic oxygens of the Si-O-Al groups in the zeolite framework provide heterolytic dissociative adsorption of propane, offering Zn-propyl species and acid protons of the Si-OH-Al group.\n\nThe modification of zeolites with zinc changes the reaction mechanism, as evidenced by the regioselectivity of hydrogen/deuterium exchange observed in Zn/H-MFI compared to unmodified H-MFI. The dehydrogenation of propane to form carbenium ions can occur on either zinc cations or Lewis sites of subnanometric ZnO clusters.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of metal-exchanged zeolites by allowing for more selective and efficient alkane transformations, which are crucial in various industrial processes such as the production of valuable petrochemicals and fuels. {"Quotes": "[1] \"The initial step in propane activation over Zn/H-MFI is dissociative adsorption over zinc oxide sites, leading to the formation of propyl zinc species and ZnOH groups.\"\n\n[2] \"At low partial pressure of the reactants, H2 recombinative desorption and hydrogen evolution is favoured, whereas at high pressures, implied under NMR batch conditions, the hydrogenolysis route is preferred.\"\n\n[3] \"The mechanism proposed for propane activation involves propane dissociation, followed by propene evolution and recombinative desorption of dihydrogen. Propene further oligomerizes over acidic sites, while dihydrogen either evolves as a final product or participates in propane hydrogenolysis into ethane and methane.\"\n\n[4] \"This mechanistic pathway explains the higher activity of Zn/H-MFI catalysts with respect to H-MFI and the lower selectivity to aromatics in comparison to Ga/H-MFI.\"\n\n[5] \"The development of in situ MAS NMR spectroscopic techniques [11] has allowed monitoring of the catalytic transformations directly on a catalyst surface during the course of the reaction.\"\n\n[6] \"Application of in situ 13C MAS NMR spectroscopy has enabled identification of the proposed detailed bifunctional mechanism of propane activation over Ga-modified acidic MFI zeolite [11a,11c,11e,12].\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly Zn/H-MFI, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations:\n\n1. Composition: The presence of zinc oxide sites and acidic sites in the catalyst enables a bifunctional mechanism for propane activation and subsequent reactions. The high zinc content leads to the formation of zinc propyl and ZnOH groups at the onset of the propane reaction.\n\n2. Structure: The MFI zeolite framework provides a suitable environment for both zinc oxide species and acidic sites, allowing for the dissociative adsorption of propane and further oligomerization of propene.\n\n3. Reaction conditions: \n   - Pressure plays a crucial role in determining the reaction pathway. Low partial pressures favor hydrogen evolution, while high pressures promote the hydrogenolysis route.\n   - Temperature affects the reaction, with Zn/H-MFI catalysts requiring higher temperatures compared to other metal-exchanged zeolites due to the weaker strength of zinc oxide species.\n\nThe mechanism involves initial dissociative adsorption of propane on zinc oxide sites, followed by propene evolution and recombinative desorption of dihydrogen. Propene then oligomerizes over acidic sites, while dihydrogen either evolves as a final product or participates in propane hydrogenolysis into ethane and methane.\n\nThis mechanistic pathway contributes to the industrial significance of Zn/H-MFI catalysts by providing higher activity compared to H-MFI catalysts. However, it also results in lower selectivity to aromatics compared to Ga/H-MFI catalysts due to enhanced ethane and methane formation through hydrogenolysis.\n\nThe development of in situ MAS NMR spectroscopic techniques has allowed for direct monitoring of catalytic transformations on the catalyst surface during reactions, enabling detailed identification of the bifunctional mechanism in metal-modified zeolites.\n\nWhile the provided information doesn't specifically discuss oxidative dehydrogenation or supported vanadium oxides, the insights gained from the Zn/H-MFI system demonstrate how metal-based catalysts can be tailored to achieve desired alkane transformations. The balance between metal sites (for initial activation) and acid sites (for further transformations) appears to be a key factor in determining catalyst performance and product selectivity in industrial processes.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.jcat.2005.11.037"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes provides a thermodynamically accessible route to the synthesis of alkenes from alkanes (13). Supported vanadium oxides (VOx) catalyze this reaction, but unselective combustion pathways limit alkene selectivities, especially at high conversions (418).\"\n\n[2] \"The extent of dispersion and the local and extended structure of supported VOx species depend on the chemical identity and the surface area of the support (13, 17, 18).\"\n\n[3] \"Polyvanadate species that form on ZrO2 at VOx surface densities above 34 VOx/nm2 provide active and selective sites for oxidative dehydrogenation reactions. Catalysts with lower VOx surface density and uncovered ZrO2 surfaces show lower alkene selectivities because of competing oxidation pathways leading to undesired CO and CO2 products.\"\n\n[4] \"Initial propene selectivities increase with increasing VOx surface density, as monovanadate species and exposed support sites, which favor primary combustion pathways, decrease in concentration.\"\n\n[5] \"Oxidative dehydrogenation rates per V-atom reach a maximum on VOx domains of intermediate size, which provide a balance between the activity of surface VOx species and their accessibility to reactants.\"\n\n[6] \"Interactions with supports determine the type of VOx structures present at a given surface density, but turnover rates do not depend on the identity of the support when differences in VOx structure are taken into account.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of supported vanadium oxide catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in oxidative dehydrogenation processes. Key factors include:\n\n1. Catalyst composition: The type of support (e.g., ZrO2, Al2O3, SiO2) and vanadium loading affect the dispersion and structure of VOx species.\n\n2. VOx surface density: Higher surface densities generally lead to the formation of polyvanadate species, which are more active and selective for oxidative dehydrogenation. Optimal rates occur at intermediate densities that balance activity and reactant accessibility.\n\n3. Support interactions: The chemical identity of the support influences the dispersion and structure of VOx species at a given surface density. However, turnover rates do not depend on the support identity when VOx structural differences are accounted for.\n\n4. VOx structure: The evolution from isolated monovanadates to polyvanadate domains affects catalytic behavior. Polyvanadate species are generally more selective for alkene formation.\n\n5. Reaction conditions: High conversions can lead to unselective combustion pathways, limiting alkene selectivity.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of supported vanadium oxide catalysts by:\n\n1. Allowing tuning of catalyst properties to optimize alkene yield and selectivity.\n2. Providing a means to balance the competing requirements of activity and selectivity.\n3. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Zeolites are crystalline alumosilicates and as such are of no interest for redox reactions. Unlike other oxides, they have an intracrystalline system of micropores of molecular dimensions. Chemical composition of zeolites can be modified by transition metals due to their incorporation either into the crystal lattice or into the micropore space.\"\n\n[2] \"Modification with Fe is a special case. Unlike other metals, whose oxides initially exhibit some activity of the same sort as that after the introduction into the zeolite, iron atoms in ZSM-5 matrix acquire fundamentally new features compared to the free Fe2O3.\"\n\n[3] \"The iron loses its ability to activate O2, but acquires special affinity towards nitrous oxide, causing its decomposition in a particular way [53]. This reaction gives rise to the formation of a new form of surface oxygen (called a-oxygen [54]) which, as one can see further, is behind a unique performance of FeZSM-5 zeolites in the oxidation with N2O.\"\n\n[4] \"The study of parafn partial oxidation catalysts gave different observations [44]. In this case, to avoid overoxidation, the active sites (usually vanadium ions) must be isolated in the inert matrix of the support to prevent oxygen diffusion from the neighboring sites.\"\n\n[5] \"A detailed study of vanadium oxide supported on a variety of carriers [45] concluded that the presence of VOV fragments was essential for the selective oxidation.\"\n\n[6] \"Bulk oxides with the oxygen of low lability (MgO, ZnO, TiO2, etc.), or silica-supported oxides of V, Mo, W are usually used for this purpose.\"\n\n[7] \"The distribution of iron and other metals in zeolites has been studied extensively. In general, Fe can occupy three positions in ZSM-5 matrix [8487] and can exist as:\n1. Isolated ions in tetrahedral positions of the crystal lattice;\n2. Isolated ions or small complexes inside the intracrystalline micropore space;\n3. {"Quotes": "[1] \"The zinc-exchanged (ZSM-5-Zn) and proton-exchanged (ZSM-5-H) forms of ZSM-5 zeolite have been studied for the conversion of propane to aromatic hydrocarbons at temperatures in the range 730-820 K.\"\n\n[2] \"ZSM-5-Zn was the more active and gave the better BTX selectivity (60-7OC%).\"\n\n[3] \"The main features of the conversion mechanism are proposed involving propane dehydrogenation, followed by oligomerization and aromatization.\"\n\n[4] \"The primary dehydrogenation reaction is mechanistically different on the two types of catalyst: over ZSM-5-H dehydrogenation is rate controlled, while over ZSM-5-Zn this step is in quasi-equilibrium.\"\n\n[5] \"We suggest that this effect arises because the respective dehydrogenation steps are in quasi-equilibrium, with reaction 4 controlling the conversion and a dehydrogenation between aromatic and cyclic precursor controlling the BTX selectivity (both processes may well involve species of the type [Zn-H]+, c.f. reaction 4.\"\n\n[6] \"In the case of the ZSM-5-Zn catalyst, we suggest the zinc cation acts as a hydride acceptor to give a transient species such as [Zn-HI+ (and thence hydrogen)\"\n\n[7] \"Over both ZSM-5-H and ZSM-5-Zn catalysts, ethane reactant was found to be much less reactive than propane: at 870 K the extent of ethane conversion was < 5%.\"\n\n[8] \"With both catalysts, as expected, the propane conversion fell with increasing space velocity but the product distributions behaved differently.\"\n\n[9] \"The apparent activation energies, obtained from Arrhenius plots of the propane conversions, were ZSM-5-Zn (I.28 wt% aluminium), 121+15 kJ mol-'; ZSM-5-Zn (0.71 wt% aluminium), 104+15 kJ mol-'; ZSM-5-H (1.28 wt% aluminium), 120t15 kJ mol-'.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in zeolite-catalyzed reactions. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Application of alternative oxidants in the form of various oxygen-containing molecules opens new possibilities. The idea of this approach is as follows. Variation of the oxidant chemistry should change the state of surface oxygen, while the activation of organic molecules may remain unchanged or affected to a smaller degree, thus providing new opportunity for controlling the selectivity.\"\n\n[2] \"In 1983, Iwamoto et al. [7] were the first to use N2O for the oxidation of benzene: [reaction equation] This reaction over a vanadia catalyst allowed achieving much better selectivity as compared to the oxidation by dioxygen. At 550 8C, the reaction selectivity exceeded 70%.\"\n\n[3] \"As for the nature of the catalytic activity, two different approaches were suggested. One of them is based on a traditional notion that zeolites are the acidic catalysts with strong Brønsted and Lewis sites. The other one relates to the common oxidation catalysis, which might be provided by the presence of a transition metal admixture.\"\n\n[4] \"Presently, one may conclude that the iron idea seems to be generally accepted, although the detailed structure and composition of α-sites till now are not perfectly clear and thus are the subject of many theoretical [54–60] and experimental [58,61–69] studies.\"\n\n[5] \"The reduced iron atoms of complex (III) appear to be inert to dioxygen. However, they are readily oxidized at elevated temperatures (over 200 8C) by nitrous oxide into complex (IV) to give adsorbed O a species. Namely, this reversible redox transition Fe2+ ↔ Fe3+ between complexes (III) and (IV) is considered to provide a selective transfer of oxygen atoms from N2O to hydrocarbon molecules to be oxidized.\"\n\n[6] \"Presently, many researchers are trying to use N2O for conducting various oxidation reactions by the catalytic way at elevated temperature. Quite good selectivities were achieved in the oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes [40,42,43] and in the epoxidation of propylene to propylene oxide [86].\"\n\n[7] \"To suppress such side transformations and especially coking, leading to a heavy problem of the catalyst deactivation, the researchers are trying to neutralize various functional groups on the zeolite surface but leaving intact the α-sites. This is a difficult challenge.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations. For supported vanadium oxides, the use of alternative oxidants like N2O can change the state of surface oxygen, allowing better control of selectivity compared to traditional O2 oxidation. This was demonstrated in the oxidation of benzene using vanadia catalysts, where N2O achieved much higher selectivity than O2 at 550°C.\n\nFor metal-exchanged zeolites, particularly iron-containing ones, the catalytic activity stems from either the acidic nature of zeolites (Brønsted and Lewis sites) or the presence of transition metal admixtures. The iron forms specific active sites called α-sites within the zeolite structure, which are responsible for the selective transfer of oxygen atoms from N2O to hydrocarbon molecules. The reaction mechanism involves a reversible redox transition between Fe2+ and Fe3+ states, occurring at elevated temperatures (over 200°C) in the presence of N2O.\n\nReaction conditions, especially temperature, play a crucial role in determining efficiency and selectivity. At elevated temperatures, N2O can be used for oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes and epoxidation reactions with good selectivities. However, high temperatures can also lead to side reactions and coking, causing catalyst deactivation.\n\nThe industrial significance of these catalysts is influenced by their selectivity, efficiency, and stability. Researchers are working on improving catalyst performance by neutralizing certain functional groups on the zeolite surface while preserving the active α-sites. This approach aims to suppress side reactions and coking, enhancing the catalyst's longevity and industrial viability.\n\nOverall, the interplay between catalyst composition (e.g., presence of transition metals), structure (e.g., zeolite framework), and reaction conditions (e.g., temperature, oxidant choice) determines the efficiency and selectivity of alkane transformations, making these factors crucial for developing industrially significant processes.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.cattod.2004.12.012"},

The geometry of cation sites in the zeolite framework influences the stability and reactivity of gallium species.\n\n3. Reaction mechanisms: Both alkyl and carbenium mechanisms are possible for ethane activation. The carbenium mechanism shows easier subsequent transformations due to lower intermediate stability. Concerted dehydrogenation pathways compete with primary dehydrogenation routes.\n\n4. Efficiency: The overall activation energy for ethane dehydrogenation over GaO+ sites is about 25% lower than that for reduced Ga+ sites, indicating higher efficiency of the oxidized form. However, the stability of GaO+ species is limited, with estimates suggesting only about three catalytic cycles before reduction to Ga+.\n\n5. Catalyst stability: The oxidized GaO+ species quickly reduce to Ga+ under reaction conditions, limiting long-term stability. Low stability of isolated gallyl ions may lead to oligomerization, forming various gallium oxide clusters.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of metal-based catalysts by influencing their activity, selectivity, and longevity. The high initial activity of oxidized catalysts makes them attractive for industrial applications. However, the rapid deactivation and complex behavior of gallium species present challenges for maintaining long-term catalytic performance, requiring careful control of reaction conditions or periodic regeneration.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp072110z"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"We examine possible reaction mechanisms for the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by supported transition metal oxide catalysts. Specifically we are interested in V2O5 supported on SiO22,12,13 and consider monomeric OV(O)3 surface species, which together with dimeric and oligomeric V2O5 species are present on catalyst surfaces with low V2O5 loadings.\"\n\n[2] \"Hydrogen abstraction by the vanadyl oxygen atom is the initial step in the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by supported vanadium oxide species. The vanadium surface coverage is crucial, with optimal activity observed below 3 V/nm2, before V2O5 crystallites start to form.\n3. The active sites are modeled as vanadyl-substituted silsesquioxane, representing monomeric vanadium oxide species on silica.\n\nReaction Mechanism:\n1. The initial step involves hydrogen abstraction by the vanadyl (OVV) oxygen atom, forming a diradical intermediate (C3H7•·HOVIV(O)3).\n2. The first hydrogen abstraction occurs exclusively on OVV sites, while the second can also happen on VOSi bridging oxygen sites.\n3. Desorption of propyl radicals leads to a stationary concentration in the gas phase and leaves reduced HOVIV sites on the surface.\n4. Fast reoxidation maintains a low concentration of reduced sites compared to OVV sites.\n\nReaction Conditions and Efficiency:\n1. Temperature significantly affects the reaction kinetics. At 750 K, the apparent activation energy is predicted to be 123 ± 5 kJ/mol, with a rate constant of about 0.26 s-1.\n2. For V2O5/SiO2 catalysts with vanadium surface coverages below 3 V/nm2, turnover frequencies (TOF) of around 0.01 s-1 at 748 K and 0.3 × 10-3 s-1 at 623 K have been observed for propene formation.\n\nIndustrial Significance:\n1. The ability to fine-tune catalyst composition, structure, and reaction conditions allows for optimization of catalyst performance.\n2. Understanding the reaction mechanism and kinetics helps in designing more efficient catalysts for industrial applications.\n3. The comparison between supported monomers and bulk V2O5 surfaces suggests potential for improving catalyst activity, although accessibility of active sites in three-dimensional particles may limit apparent turnover frequencies.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial importance of supported vanadium oxide catalysts in alkane transformation processes, particularly in the production of valuable chemical intermediates from alkanes.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp071409e"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Supported vanadium oxide catalysts, containing surface vanadia species on oxide supports, are extensively employed as catalysts for many hydrocarbon oxidation reactions.\"\n\n[2] \"The molecular structures of these surface vanadia species are tentatively thought to consist of a terminal V=O bond and three bridging V-O-support bonds for the isolated species, and a terminal V=O bond with one bridging V-O-support and two bridging V-O-V bonds for the polymerized species.\"\n\n[3] \"The bridging V-O-support bond appears to be associated with the critical oxygen required for hydrocarbon oxidation reactions since changing the specific oxide support dramatically affects the TOF: approximately four orders of magnitude for methanol oxidation to formaldehyde and two-orders of magnitude for butane oxidation to maleic anhydride.\"\n\n[4] \"The general trend appears to be CeOz>ZrO2 TiO2>NbzOs>A12Os>SiO2, which inversely correlates with the Sanderson electronegativity of the oxide support cations.\"\n\n[5] \"As a result of these physical and chemical characteristics, supported vanadia catalysts tend to be among the most active and selective supported metal oxide catalysts for hydrocarbon oxidation reactions.\"\n\n[6] \"The oxidative dehydrogenation of propane to propylene over V2O5/Nb2O5 was not dependent on the concentration of the bridging V-O-V bonds\"\n\n[7] \"Oxidation reactions requiring only one surface vanadia sites are generally not sensitive to the surface vanadia coverage and the presence of noninteracting metal oxide additives. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Ga/H-ZSM-5 catalysts are most active and selective for the aromatisation of propane [1]. It is widely accepted that their activity and selectivity result from their bifunctional properties, whereby gallium species are responsible for their dehydrogenation-hydrogenation activity and the Brönsted acid sites of the zeolite provide the centres necessary for chain growth.\"\n\n[2] \"Recently, it was shown that dual active sites are responsible for the activation of propane on Ga/H-ZSM-5 [2-4]. Subsequent oxygen treatment enhances further the activity and aromatics selectivity and has little effect on methane selectivity.\"\n\n[5] \"These improvements of catalytic performance are explained by gallium migration in reducing conditions. Key factors include:\n\n1. Composition: The bifunctional nature of Ga/H-ZSM-5 catalysts, with gallium species responsible for dehydrogenation-hydrogenation activity and Brönsted acid sites from the zeolite enabling chain growth, is crucial for their high activity and selectivity.\n\n2. Structure: Dual active sites consisting of highly dispersed (Ga3+, O2-) ion pairs working synergistically with neighboring Brönsted sites are responsible for propane activation. The dispersion of gallium species within the zeolite structure is vital for catalyst performance.\n\n3. Reaction conditions:\n   a) Reducing conditions (H2 treatment) increase gallium dispersion, leading to higher activity and aromatics selectivity while reducing methane formation.\n   b) Oxidative conditions (O2 treatment) following H2 treatment further boost activity and aromatics selectivity without affecting methane selectivity.\n   c) Hydrogen can have an inhibitory effect on propane transformation, possibly due to its impact on the rate-limiting step of propane dehydrogenation.\n\n4. Catalyst activation: H2-O2 pretreatment cycles dramatically improve catalytic performance by optimizing gallium dispersion and oxidation state. This pretreatment eliminates the induction period and could be industrially significant for preactivating and stabilizing catalysts before use in aromatization units.\n\n5. Reaction-regeneration cycles: The migration of gallium species in and out of the zeolite intracrystalline void space during reaction (reducing) and regeneration (oxidative) cycles affects catalyst performance over time.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of these catalysts by enhancing their activity, selectivity, and stability in alkane transformation processes, particularly in propane aromatization. The ability to fine-tune catalyst properties through composition, structure, and pretreatment conditions allows for optimized performance in industrial-scale applications.", "Doi": "10.1016/s0920-5861(96)00015-6"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Isolated molybdate species supported on silica are reported to have the highest specific activity and selectivity for the direct oxidation of methane to formaldehyde.\"\n\n[2] \"On the basis of evidence from extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) and Raman spectroscopy, the Mo atoms in this catalyst are present as isolated, pentacoordinated molybdate species containing a single MoO bond.\"\n\n[3] \"It is proposed that peroxides are produced by the reaction of O2 with a small concentration of reduced molybdate species and that the reaction of CH4 with these peroxide species leads to the formation of formaldehyde.\"\n\n[4] \"Low concentrations of H2O were observed to enhance the activity of MoOx/SiO2 for CH4 oxidation to formaldehyde.\"\n\n[5] \"During CH4 oxidation to formaldehyde, the catalyst undergoes only a very small degree of reduction and typically only 50500 ppm of MoVI is reduced to MoIV.\"\n\n[6] \"It was established that treatment of MoOx/SiO2 at 873 K under pure methane reduces the dispersed molybdate species to only a limited extent and results mainly in the deposition of amorphous carbon.\"", "English": "The literature focuses on molybdenum oxide catalysts supported on silica (MoOx/SiO2) for methane oxidation to formaldehyde, rather than vanadium oxides or zeolites specifically. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Catalytic reactions of hydrocarbons in zeolites can be profoundly affected by extraframework transition-metal cationic clusters. CO is the main product of propene oxidation and its formation increases with decreasing dispersion of active sites.\"\n\n[6] \"Vanadium species formed on the alumina support after calcination between 773 and 973 K at vanadium loadings between 4.45 and 13.2 wt.% were characterized with different methods (51V NMR, ESR, H2-TPR and in situ UVvis-DRS) and tested in the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane.\"\n\n[7] \"By calcination at 873 K, beside dispersed isolated and polymerized V5+ surface species mainly in tetrahedral coordination, a crystalline AlVO4 phase was formed. {"Quotes": "[1] \"The initial step in propane activation over Zn/H-MFI is dissociative adsorption over zinc oxide sites, leading to the formation of propyl zinc species and ZnOH groups.\"\n\n[2] \"At low partial pressure of the reactants, H2 recombinative desorption and hydrogen evolution is favoured, whereas at high pressures, implied under NMR batch conditions, the hydrogenolysis route is preferred.\"\n\n[3] \"The mechanism proposed for propane activation involves propane dissociation, followed by propene evolution and recombinative desorption of dihydrogen. Propene further oligomerizes over acidic sites, while dihydrogen either evolves as a final product or participates in propane hydrogenolysis into ethane and methane.\"\n\n[4] \"This mechanistic pathway explains the higher activity of Zn/H-MFI catalysts with respect to H-MFI and the lower selectivity to aromatics in comparison to Ga/H-MFI.\"\n\n[5] \"The development of in situ MAS NMR spectroscopic techniques [11] has allowed monitoring of the catalytic transformations directly on a catalyst surface during the course of the reaction.\"\n\n[6] \"Application of in situ 13C MAS NMR spectroscopy has enabled identification of the proposed detailed bifunctional mechanism of propane activation over Ga-modified acidic MFI zeolite [11a,11c,11e,12].\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly Zn/H-MFI, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations:\n\n1. Composition: The presence of zinc oxide sites and acidic sites in the catalyst enables a bifunctional mechanism for propane activation and subsequent reactions. The high zinc content leads to the formation of zinc propyl and ZnOH groups at the onset of the propane reaction.\n\n2. Structure: The MFI zeolite framework provides a suitable environment for both zinc oxide species and acidic sites, allowing for the dissociative adsorption of propane and further oligomerization of propene.\n\n3. Propene then oligomerizes over acidic sites, while dihydrogen either evolves as a final product or participates in propane hydrogenolysis into ethane and methane.\n\nThis mechanistic pathway contributes to the industrial significance of Zn/H-MFI catalysts by providing higher activity compared to H-MFI catalysts. {"Quotes": "[1] \"The activation of ethane over zinc- and gallium-modified HZSM-5 dehydrogenation catalysts was studied by diffuse reflectance infrared spectroscopy.\"\n\n[2] \"Hydrocarbon activation on HZSM-5 modified by bivalent Zn and univalent Ga cations proceeds via two distinctly different mechanisms.\"\n\n[3] \"The stronger molecular adsorption of ethane by the acidbase pairs formed by distantly separated cationic Zn21 and basic oxygen sites results already at room temperature in strong polarizability of adsorbed ethane and subsequent heterolytic dissociative adsorption at moderate temperatures.\"\n\n[4] \"In contrast, molecular adsorption of ethane on Ga1 cations is weak. This corresponds to the finding that ethane is more readily activated over the zincoxygen acidbase pairs than over Ga1 cations.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations in processes like oxidative dehydrogenation and zeolite-catalyzed reactions. \n\nFor metal-exchanged zeolites, specifically Zn/ZSM-5 and Ga/ZSM-5, the nature of the metal cation plays a crucial role:\n\n1. Composition: \n   - Zn/ZSM-5 contains bivalent Zn2+ cations.\n   - Ga/ZSM-5 contains univalent Ga1+ cations.\n\n2. Structure:\n   - In Zn/ZSM-5, Zn2+ cations form acid-base pairs with basic oxygen sites in the zeolite framework.\n   - In Ga/ZSM-5, Ga1+ cations are present without forming such pairs.\n\n3.
