{"Quotes": "\"The high temperature oxidation of CH4 to HCHO may be carried out over pure SiO2 and V2O5/SiO5. In the single-pass mode, the HCHO yields do not exceed 34%; however, respectable spacetime-yields (STY) of about 1200 g/kg cat h at 30% selectivity have been achieved over a 2% V2O5/SiO2 catalyst at 600C [20].\"\n\n\"Several bifunctional catalysts are known to promote this reaction, with the most thoroughly studied being a Mo/H-ZSM-5 zeolite [3538].\"\n\n\"Most investigators agree that molybdenum carbide or oxycarbide is responsible for the conversion of CH4 to C2H4 and that the C2H4 is converted to aromatic products over the acidic sites within the channels of the zeolite.\"\n\n\"A W, Zn/H-ZSM-5 catalyst, prepared by Zhang and co-workers [38] via a controlled impregnation technique, is of particular interest because a CH4 conversion of 23% at 850C, with high benzene selectivity, has been observed.\"\n\n\"Over the better catalysts, which include SrO/La2O3 [25] and Mn/Na2WO4/SiO2 [26,27], a C2 selectivity of about 80% can be achieved at a CH4 conversion of 20%.\"\n\n\"Other metals including W and Fe are effective for promoting this reaction, and there is evidence that these metals are active as suboxides, rather than as carbides [37].\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in processes like oxidative dehydrogenation and zeolite-catalyzed reactions:\n\n1. Composition: Various metal-based catalysts show different performances:\n   - Supported vanadium oxides (e.g., V2O5/SiO2) are effective for methane oxidation to formaldehyde.\n   - Mo/H-ZSM-5 zeolites are well-studied for methane conversion to aromatics.\n   - W, Zn/H-ZSM-5 catalysts show high methane conversion and benzene selectivity.\n   - Other effective catalysts include SrO/La2O3 and Mn/Na2WO4/SiO2 for C2 selectivity.\n   - W and Fe are also effective, possibly active as suboxides.\n\n2. Structure: \n   - In zeolites, the acidic sites within the channels are crucial for converting intermediates (e.g., C2H4) to final products (e.g., aromatics).\n   - The bifunctional nature of catalysts like Mo/H-ZSM-5 allows for multiple reaction steps.\n\n3. Reaction conditions:\n   - High temperatures are often required, e.g., 600°C for V2O5/SiO2 and 850°C for W, Zn/H-ZSM-5.\n   - Conversion rates and selectivity are highly dependent on temperature.\n\n4. Mechanisms:\n   - In Mo/H-ZSM-5, molybdenum carbide or oxycarbide converts CH4 to C2H4, which is then converted to aromatics on acidic sites.\n   - Different metals may be active in different forms (e.g., carbides, oxycarbides, or suboxides).\n\n5. Efficiency:\n   - V2O5/SiO2 achieves space-time yields of about 1200 g/kg cat h at 30% selectivity.\n   - W, Zn/H-ZSM-5 shows 23% methane conversion with high benzene selectivity.\n   - Some catalysts achieve 80% C2 selectivity at 20% CH4 conversion.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of these catalysts by enabling higher conversions, improved selectivities, and the potential for more efficient processes in converting abundant alkanes into valuable chemicals and fuels. However, the high temperatures required and the complexity of catalyst design present ongoing challenges for industrial application.", "Doi": "10.1016/s0920-5861(00)00456-9"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes provides a thermodynamically accessible route to the synthesis of alkenes from alkanes (13). Supported vanadium oxides (VOx) catalyze this reaction, but unselective combustion pathways limit alkene selectivities, especially at high conversions (418).\"\n\n[2] \"The extent of dispersion and the local and extended structure of supported VOx species depend on the chemical identity and the surface area of the support (13, 17, 18).\"\n\n[3] \"Polyvanadate species that form on ZrO2 at VOx surface densities above 34 VOx/nm2 provide active and selective sites for oxidative dehydrogenation reactions. Catalysts with lower VOx surface density and uncovered ZrO2 surfaces show lower alkene selectivities because of competing oxidation pathways leading to undesired CO and CO2 products.\"\n\n[4] \"Initial propene selectivities increase with increasing VOx surface density, as monovanadate species and exposed support sites, which favor primary combustion pathways, decrease in concentration.\"\n\n[5] \"Oxidative dehydrogenation rates per V-atom reach a maximum on VOx domains of intermediate size, which provide a balance between the activity of surface VOx species and their accessibility to reactants.\"\n\n[6] \"Interactions with supports determine the type of VOx structures present at a given surface density, but turnover rates do not depend on the identity of the support when differences in VOx structure are taken into account.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of supported vanadium oxide catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in oxidative dehydrogenation processes. Key factors include:\n\n1. Catalyst composition: The type of support (e.g., ZrO2, Al2O3, SiO2) and vanadium loading affect the dispersion and structure of VOx species.\n\n2. VOx surface density: Higher surface densities generally lead to the formation of polyvanadate species, which are more active and selective for oxidative dehydrogenation. Optimal rates occur at intermediate densities that balance activity and reactant accessibility.\n\n3. Support interactions: The chemical identity of the support influences the dispersion and structure of VOx species at a given surface density. However, turnover rates do not depend on the support identity when VOx structural differences are accounted for.\n\n4. VOx structure: The evolution from isolated monovanadates to polyvanadate domains affects catalytic behavior. Polyvanadate species are generally more selective for alkene formation.\n\n5. Reaction conditions: High conversions can lead to unselective combustion pathways, limiting alkene selectivity.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of supported vanadium oxide catalysts by:\n\n1. Allowing tuning of catalyst properties to optimize alkene yield and selectivity.\n2. Providing a means to balance the competing requirements of activity and selectivity.\n3. Enabling control of VOx structure and dispersion through support selection and surface density manipulation.\n\nHowever, challenges remain in maximizing alkene yields, particularly at high conversions, due to competing combustion reactions. The study does not directly address metal-exchanged zeolites or their specific industrial significance in alkane transformations.", "Doi": "10.1006/jcat.1998.2295"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Zeolites are crystalline alumosilicates and as such are of no interest for redox reactions. Unlike other oxides, they have an intracrystalline system of micropores of molecular dimensions. Chemical composition of zeolites can be modified by transition metals due to their incorporation either into the crystal lattice or into the micropore space.\"\n\n[2] \"Modification with Fe is a special case. Unlike other metals, whose oxides initially exhibit some activity of the same sort as that after the introduction into the zeolite, iron atoms in ZSM-5 matrix acquire fundamentally new features compared to the free Fe2O3.\"\n\n[3] \"The iron loses its ability to activate O2, but acquires special affinity towards nitrous oxide, causing its decomposition in a particular way [53]. This reaction gives rise to the formation of a new form of surface oxygen (called a-oxygen [54]) which, as one can see further, is behind a unique performance of FeZSM-5 zeolites in the oxidation with N2O.\"\n\n[4] \"The study of parafn partial oxidation catalysts gave different observations [44]. In this case, to avoid overoxidation, the active sites (usually vanadium ions) must be isolated in the inert matrix of the support to prevent oxygen diffusion from the neighboring sites.\"\n\n[5] \"A detailed study of vanadium oxide supported on a variety of carriers [45] concluded that the presence of VOV fragments was essential for the selective oxidation.\"\n\n[6] \"Bulk oxides with the oxygen of low lability (MgO, ZnO, TiO2, etc.), or silica-supported oxides of V, Mo, W are usually used for this purpose.\"\n\n[7] \"The distribution of iron and other metals in zeolites has been studied extensively. In general, Fe can occupy three positions in ZSM-5 matrix [8487] and can exist as:\n1. Isolated ions in tetrahedral positions of the crystal lattice;\n2. Isolated ions or small complexes inside the intracrystalline micropore space;\n3. Finely dispersed oxide phase on the outer surface of zeolite crystals.\"\n\n[8] \"Depending on Fe content and on the mode of catalyst activation, Ca can be regulated within several orders of magnitude, reaching up to nearly 100 mmol/g for the most active catalysts.\"\n\n[9] \"Experimentally, observed characteristics of a-oxygen can be explained by binuclear quantum chemical model of a-sites [92].\"\n\n[10] \"According to recent quantum mechanical calculations [99], methane oxidation on FeZSM-5 proceeds with participation of binuclear complexes having Fe atoms bound by (m-oxo-) and (m-hydroxo-) bridges.\"\n\n[11] \"Recently, based on successful pilot plant operation, Solutia made an announcement of the jointly developed new process and shared its plans for fast-track commercialization [113]. Typical performance data from the Pensacola pilot plant are given in Table 7.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations:\n\n1. Composition:\n   - Zeolites can be modified with transition metals, altering their chemical composition and creating active sites for redox reactions.\n   - Fe-modified ZSM-5 zeolites exhibit unique properties compared to other metal-modified zeolites or free Fe2O3.\n   - Supported vanadium oxides, particularly those with VOV fragments, are essential for selective oxidation reactions.\n\n2. Structure:\n   - Zeolites have an intracrystalline system of micropores that influence catalyst behavior.\n   - In Fe-ZSM-5, iron can occupy three positions: isolated ions in tetrahedral positions, isolated ions or small complexes in micropores, or as dispersed oxide on the outer surface.\n   - Binuclear iron complexes with oxo- and hydroxo-bridges are believed to be active in methane oxidation on FeZSM-5.\n   - For paraffin partial oxidation, vanadium ions must be isolated in an inert support matrix to prevent over-oxidation.\n\n3. Reaction conditions:\n   - Fe-ZSM-5 loses the ability to activate O2 but gains affinity for N2O decomposition, forming a unique \"α-oxygen\" species.\n   - The mode of catalyst activation affects its activity, with Fe content in FeZSM-5 influencing the concentration of active sites.\n   - Temperature and choice of oxidant (e.g., O2 vs. N2O) greatly affect reaction mechanisms and product selectivity.\n\nThese factors contribute to industrial significance by:\n- Enabling more selective and efficient alkane transformation processes.\n- Allowing for the development of novel reactions, such as the direct oxidation of benzene to phenol.\n- Providing opportunities for process optimization and commercialization, as demonstrated by Solutia's pilot plant operations.\n- Offering potential for utilizing waste products (e.g., N2O) in valuable chemical transformations.", "Doi": "10.1016/s0920-5861(98)00026-1"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"The zinc-exchanged (ZSM-5-Zn) and proton-exchanged (ZSM-5-H) forms of ZSM-5 zeolite have been studied for the conversion of propane to aromatic hydrocarbons at temperatures in the range 730-820 K.\"\n\n[2] \"ZSM-5-Zn was the more active and gave the better BTX selectivity (60-7OC%).\"\n\n[3] \"The main features of the conversion mechanism are proposed involving propane dehydrogenation, followed by oligomerization and aromatization.\"\n\n[4] \"The primary dehydrogenation reaction is mechanistically different on the two types of catalyst: over ZSM-5-H dehydrogenation is rate controlled, while over ZSM-5-Zn this step is in quasi-equilibrium.\"\n\n[5] \"We suggest that this effect arises because the respective dehydrogenation steps are in quasi-equilibrium, with reaction 4 controlling the conversion and a dehydrogenation between aromatic and cyclic precursor controlling the BTX selectivity (both processes may well involve species of the type [Zn-H]+, c.f. reaction 4.\"\n\n[6] \"In the case of the ZSM-5-Zn catalyst, we suggest the zinc cation acts as a hydride acceptor to give a transient species such as [Zn-HI+ (and thence hydrogen)\"\n\n[7] \"Over both ZSM-5-H and ZSM-5-Zn catalysts, ethane reactant was found to be much less reactive than propane: at 870 K the extent of ethane conversion was < 5%.\"\n\n[8] \"With both catalysts, as expected, the propane conversion fell with increasing space velocity but the product distributions behaved differently.\"\n\n[9] \"The apparent activation energies, obtained from Arrhenius plots of the propane conversions, were ZSM-5-Zn (I.28 wt% aluminium), 121+15 kJ mol-'; ZSM-5-Zn (0.71 wt% aluminium), 104+15 kJ mol-'; ZSM-5-H (1.28 wt% aluminium), 120t15 kJ mol-'.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations, particularly in zeolite-catalyzed reactions. Key findings include:\n\n1. Catalyst composition: Zinc-exchanged ZSM-5 (ZSM-5-Zn) shows higher activity and better BTX (benzene, toluene, xylene) selectivity compared to proton-exchanged ZSM-5 (ZSM-5-H). The zinc cation acts as a hydride acceptor, forming transient [Zn-H]+ species.\n\n2. Reaction mechanism: The process involves propane dehydrogenation, followed by oligomerization and aromatization. The primary dehydrogenation step differs mechanistically between ZSM-5-H (rate-controlled) and ZSM-5-Zn (quasi-equilibrium).\n\n3. Reaction conditions:\n   - Temperature: Efficient conversion occurs in the range of 730-820 K.\n   - Space velocity: Increasing space velocity decreases propane conversion but affects product distribution differently for each catalyst.\n   - Reactant type: Propane is much more reactive than ethane under similar conditions.\n\n4. Activation energy: Similar for both catalysts (around 120 kJ/mol), suggesting a similar overall reaction mechanism despite differences in the rate-determining step.\n\n5. Equilibrium considerations: In ZSM-5-Zn, dehydrogenation steps are in quasi-equilibrium, controlling both conversion and BTX selectivity through [Zn-H]+ species.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of metal-exchanged zeolites, particularly ZSM-5-Zn, in alkane transformation processes. The higher activity and better BTX selectivity of ZSM-5-Zn make it a promising catalyst for the production of valuable aromatic compounds from propane, which is an important industrial process. Understanding these factors can guide the development of more efficient catalysts for industrial applications in petrochemical processes.", "Doi": "10.1016/s0166-9834(00)82709-8"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Additional evidence is presented for the role of gallium reduction in preparing active Ga/ZSM-5 catalysts for alkane aromatization.\"\n\n[2] \"Although reduction generates dispersed, active gallium species in zeolite channels, it is shown that for ethane dehydrogenation to aromatics the reduced (with hydrogen) Ga/ZSM-5 catalysts are not as active or selective as are materials which have been substantially reoxidized either by air or by traces of oxygen in inert gases.\"\n\n[3] \"Optimal catalysts for ethane aromatization are prepared by reduction with hydrogen to disperse the gallium, followed by reoxidation to a dispersed Ga3+ phase.\"\n\n[4] \"A substantially reduced catalyst (Ga+/Z- ca. 1) is both less active and selective for aromatics formation. Such catalysts also show lower hydrocracking activities than either unreduced or reoxidized materials, because of the reduction in Brønsted acidity associated with the transformation to Ga+Z- sites.\"\n\n[5] \"It is generally agreed that the primary role of extra-framework gallium in these materials is to enhance rates of alkene, diene, or cycloalkene dehydrogenation\"\n\n[6] \"We propose that at the higher drying temperature used here (650 C) a small amount of Ga2O3 was reduced prior to the introduction of hydrogen, in agreement with the chemistry of bulk Ga2O3 at > 600 C\"\n\n[7] \"The activity and aromatics selectivity for the series of Ga/HMFI catalysts generally followed the order B (reduced/oxidized) > C (reduced/He) > A (reduced) > D (reduced/oxidized/reduced).\"\n\n[8] \"A combination of thermogravimetric analysis (TGA), differential scanning calorimetry (DSC), and X-ray diffraction (XRD) studies has been used to confirm that rapid reoxidation takes place at 550C. However, the reoxidation does not regenerate β-Ga2O3, but rather a higher energy, dispersed Ga3+ which appears to be optimal for production of aromatics from ethane.\"\n\n[9] \"Reoxidized catalysts are stable under the reaction conditions used here (530-570°C, low hydrocarbon partial pressures), but are not stable to re-reduction by hydrogen at higher partial pressures.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly Ga/ZSM-5, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations in processes like aromatization and dehydrogenation. Key factors include:\n\n1. Gallium reduction and reoxidation: The reduction of gallium is crucial for dispersing active species within zeolite channels. However, optimal catalysts are prepared by reduction followed by partial reoxidation, forming dispersed Ga3+ phases.\n\n2. Oxidation state: The balance between Ga3+ and Ga+ species affects catalyst performance. Fully reduced catalysts (Ga+/Z- ≈ 1) show lower activity and selectivity for aromatics formation and decreased hydrocracking activity due to reduced Brønsted acidity.\n\n3. Preparation method: The sequence of reduction and oxidation treatments significantly impacts catalyst performance. Catalysts reduced then oxidized generally show higher activity and selectivity than those only reduced or subjected to multiple reduction-oxidation cycles.\n\n4. Temperature effects: High drying temperatures (e.g., 650°C) can lead to partial reduction of Ga2O3 even before hydrogen introduction. Reoxidation at 550°C produces a high-energy, dispersed Ga3+ phase optimal for ethane aromatization.\n\n5. Reaction conditions: Catalyst stability depends on temperature and gas composition. Reoxidized catalysts are stable at 530-570°C with low hydrocarbon partial pressures but can be re-reduced at higher hydrogen partial pressures.\n\n6. Extra-framework gallium: It enhances rates of alkene, diene, or cycloalkene dehydrogenation, contributing to overall catalyst performance.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of Ga/ZSM-5 catalysts by allowing fine-tuning of catalyst properties to optimize specific reactions, such as alkane aromatization, and improve overall process efficiency.", "Doi": "10.1016/0926-860x(92)80336-b"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Generally, zeolite protonic acidity is necessary for NO reduction: In contrast to H,Ga-ZSM-5, Na,Ga-ZSM-5 was inactive. The authors concluded that a synergism does exist between Ga species and H+ enabling the catalyst to activate the reductant (methane) for NO decomposition in the presence of O2.\"\n\n[2] \"Ga-modified ZSM-5 is far better than the Cu-modified version, which is even less active than unmodified ZSM-5. Oxo cations GaO+ on exchange positions of the framework behave as Lewis acids.\"\n\n[9] \"Characteristically, IR vibration bands in both the hydroxyl region (wavenumbers beyond 3000 cm-1) and the framework region (wavenumbers below 1500 cm-1) are sensitive to the composition of the framework.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly supported vanadium oxides and metal-exchanged zeolites, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Ga and Zn species increase the rates of propane chemical conversion, recombinative hydrogen desorption, and deuterium incorporation from D2 into reaction products.\"\n\n[2] \"Disposal of hydrogen formed in C-H bond activation steps occurs by transfer of H-atoms to unsaturated species to form alkanes or to Ga and Zn species, which catalyze the recombinative desorption of H-atoms to form dihydrogen (H2).\"\n\n[3] \"In-situ X-ray absorption studies suggest that Ga and Zn species reside at cation exchange sites as monomeric cations and that recombinative desorption involve reduction-oxidation cycles of such cations during each dehydrocyclodimerization turnover.\"\n\n[4] \"The addition of metal cations also increases the deuterium content in 'unreacted' propane (Fig. 18). The rate of incorporation of D-atoms into 'unreacted' propane provides an independent measure of the D-content in the surface pool\"\n\n[5] \"Clearly, Ga and Zn species increase the reversibility of H-atom recombinative desorption steps and act as efficient 'portholes' for the removal of H-atoms produced during C-H bond activation steps.\"\n\n[6] \"The rate of propane dehydrocyclodimerization to form C6+ aromatics is limited by a sequence of irreversible dehydrogenation reactions leading to propene, higher alkenes, dienes, trienes, and aromatics.\"\n\n[7] \"Ga and Zn ions increase the rate of removal of alkenes from these oligomerization-cracking cycles. This removal must occur by the formation of products, such as aromatics (and H2) and alkanes, which are much less reactive than alkenes in acid-catalyzed reactions.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly metal-exchanged zeolites like Ga/H-ZSM5 and Zn/H-ZSM5, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations in processes such as oxidative dehydrogenation and zeolite-catalyzed reactions.\n\nComposition: The addition of Ga and Zn to H-ZSM5 increases the rates of propane chemical conversion, recombinative hydrogen desorption, and deuterium incorporation from D2 into reaction products. These metal species act as efficient \"portholes\" for the removal of H-atoms produced during C-H bond activation steps.\n\nStructure: In-situ X-ray absorption studies show that Ga and Zn species reside at cation exchange sites as monomeric cations within the zeolite structure. This positioning allows them to effectively catalyze the recombinative desorption of H-atoms to form dihydrogen (H2).\n\nReaction mechanism: The metal cations undergo reduction-oxidation cycles during each dehydrocyclodimerization turnover. They increase the reversibility of H-atom recombinative desorption steps and enhance the rate of removal of alkenes from oligomerization-cracking cycles, promoting the formation of more stable products like aromatics and H2.\n\nReaction conditions: The presence of deuterium in isotope studies reveals the mechanism of these catalysts. The incorporation of deuterium into reaction products and unreacted propane demonstrates the reversibility of hydrogen adsorption/desorption steps on metal-modified zeolites.\n\nEfficiency: The presence of Ga and Zn increases the overall efficiency of alkane transformations by providing an alternate pathway for hydrogen disposal. This prevents unwanted side reactions and increases the selectivity towards desired products.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial significance of metal-exchanged zeolites in alkane transformation processes. The catalytically active species are primarily ionic Zn2+ cations.\n\n          Structure: Zn2+ ions replace protons in the zeolite framework, creating Zn-O-• pairs that act as active sites for alkane activation. {"Quotes": "[1] \"Application of alternative oxidants in the form of various oxygen-containing molecules opens new possibilities. The idea of this approach is as follows. Variation of the oxidant chemistry should change the state of surface oxygen, while the activation of organic molecules may remain unchanged or affected to a smaller degree, thus providing new opportunity for controlling the selectivity.\"\n\n[2] \"In 1983, Iwamoto et al. [7] were the first to use N2O for the oxidation of benzene: [reaction equation] This reaction over a vanadia catalyst allowed achieving much better selectivity as compared to the oxidation by dioxygen. At 550 8C, the reaction selectivity exceeded 70%.\"\n\n[3] \"As for the nature of the catalytic activity, two different approaches were suggested. One of them is based on a traditional notion that zeolites are the acidic catalysts with strong Brønsted and Lewis sites. The other one relates to the common oxidation catalysis, which might be provided by the presence of a transition metal admixture.\"\n\n[4] \"Presently, one may conclude that the iron idea seems to be generally accepted, although the detailed structure and composition of α-sites till now are not perfectly clear and thus are the subject of many theoretical [54–60] and experimental [58,61–69] studies.\"\n\n[5] \"The reduced iron atoms of complex (III) appear to be inert to dioxygen. However, they are readily oxidized at elevated temperatures (over 200 8C) by nitrous oxide into complex (IV) to give adsorbed O a species. Namely, this reversible redox transition Fe2+ ↔ Fe3+ between complexes (III) and (IV) is considered to provide a selective transfer of oxygen atoms from N2O to hydrocarbon molecules to be oxidized.\"\n\n[6] \"Presently, many researchers are trying to use N2O for conducting various oxidation reactions by the catalytic way at elevated temperature. Quite good selectivities were achieved in the oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes [40,42,43] and in the epoxidation of propylene to propylene oxide [86].\"\n\n[7] \"To suppress such side transformations and especially coking, leading to a heavy problem of the catalyst deactivation, the researchers are trying to neutralize various functional groups on the zeolite surface but leaving intact the α-sites. This is a difficult challenge.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations. For supported vanadium oxides, the use of alternative oxidants like N2O can change the state of surface oxygen, allowing better control of selectivity compared to traditional O2 oxidation. This was demonstrated in the oxidation of benzene using vanadia catalysts, where N2O achieved much higher selectivity than O2 at 550°C.\n\nFor metal-exchanged zeolites, particularly iron-containing ones, the catalytic activity stems from either the acidic nature of zeolites (Brønsted and Lewis sites) or the presence of transition metal admixtures. The iron forms specific active sites called α-sites within the zeolite structure, which are responsible for the selective transfer of oxygen atoms from N2O to hydrocarbon molecules. The reaction mechanism involves a reversible redox transition between Fe2+ and Fe3+ states, occurring at elevated temperatures (over 200°C) in the presence of N2O.\n\nReaction conditions, especially temperature, play a crucial role in determining efficiency and selectivity. At elevated temperatures, N2O can be used for oxidative dehydrogenation of alkanes and epoxidation reactions with good selectivities. However, high temperatures can also lead to side reactions and coking, causing catalyst deactivation.\n\nThe industrial significance of these catalysts is influenced by their selectivity, efficiency, and stability. Researchers are working on improving catalyst performance by neutralizing certain functional groups on the zeolite surface while preserving the active α-sites. This approach aims to suppress side reactions and coking, enhancing the catalyst's longevity and industrial viability.\n\nOverall, the interplay between catalyst composition (e.g., presence of transition metals), structure (e.g., zeolite framework), and reaction conditions (e.g., temperature, oxidant choice) determines the efficiency and selectivity of alkane transformations, making these factors crucial for developing industrially significant processes.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.cattod.2004.12.012"},

{"Quotes": "[1] \"Supported vanadium oxides are particularly noteworthy because the active sites possibly involve isolated VO species (in contrast to the other systems based on MoOx or WOx).\"\n\n[2] \"Both too low and too high surface V densities are detrimental to high selectivities, and this has been associated with the combustion of the thus-formed alkenes into COx on the support and/or the polymeric V species.\"\n\n[3] \"Overall, silica can be regarded as the optimal support in terms of selectivity. {"Quotes": "[1] \"γ -Al2O3-supported vanadium oxide catalysts (06.3 wt% V atoms) were prepared, characterized, and tested for the oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) of both ethane and n-butane.\"\n\n[2] \"While the catalytic activity in the ODH reactions increases with the vanadium loading, the selectivity to the corresponding olefins shows a maximum for catalysts with 34 wt% V atoms.\"\n\n[3] \"Associated VO4 tetrahedra were observed by 51V-NMR on catalysts with V loading lower than 5 wt%, while VO6 octahedra were predominant on catalysts with higher V loading.\"\n\n[4] \"Although catalysts with octahedral V5+ species show higher catalytic activity, higher selectivity to oxydehydrogenation reactions was achieved on catalysts in which mainly tetrahedral V5+ species were observed.\"\n\n[5] \"The number and nature of the acid sites also change with the vanadium loading. Composition: Vanadium loading (0-6.3 wt% V atoms) on γ-Al2O3 support affects catalytic behavior. Higher vanadium loading increases catalytic activity, while optimal selectivity for olefins is achieved at 3-4 wt% V atoms.\n\n2. Structure: Vanadium species structure changes with loading. {"Quotes": "[1] \"We examine possible reaction mechanisms for the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by supported transition metal oxide catalysts. Specifically we are interested in V2O5 supported on SiO22,12,13 and consider monomeric OV(O)3 surface species, which together with dimeric and oligomeric V2O5 species are present on catalyst surfaces with low V2O5 loadings.\"\n\n[2] \"Hydrogen abstraction by the vanadyl oxygen atom is the initial step in the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by supported vanadium oxide species. The diradical intermediate that is reached via the diradicaloid transition structure Ts1 consists of a propyl radical attached to a HOV(O)3 site and has similar energies in the singlet and triplet states (Int2).\"\n\n[3] \"For V2O5/SiO2 catalysts with vanadium surface coverages below 3 V/nm2 (i.e., below the limit at which V2O5 crystallites start to appear) turnover frequencies (TOF) of around 0.01 s-1 (748 K) 37 and 0.3 × 10-3 s-1 (623 K) 38 have been observed for propene formation.\"\n\n[4] \"Comparison with recent DFT calculations for V2O5(001) crystal surfaces9 indicates that vanadia sites may be significantly more active in the ODH of propane if they are part of the bulk surface and not supported as monomers on silica. However, apparent turnover frequencies may be lower for V2O5 crystallites on the surface because only a fraction of sites is accessible in three-dimensional particles.\"\n\n[5] \"The initial step is hydrogen abstraction from propane by the vanadyl oxygen atom leading to a diradical intermediate, namely, C3H7•·HOVIV(O)3.\"\n\n[6] \"We analyzed possible reaction mechanisms for the oxidative dehydrogenation of propane by monomeric vanadium oxide species on silica.\"\n\n[7] \"Desorption of propyl radicals leads to a stationary concentration of propyl in the gas phase and leaves reduced HOVIV(O)3 sites on the surface. Due to fast reoxidation their concentration is much smaller than the concentration of OVV sites (Int0).\"\n\n[8] \"We investigate possible mechanisms of oxidative dehydrogenation of propane using density functional theory. Monomeric vanadium oxide species supported on silica are modeled by vanadyl-substituted silsesquioxane.\"\n\n[9] \"The initial CH bond activation step is hydrogen abstraction by the vanadyl (OVV) group yielding a diradical intermediate in which a propyl radical is bound to a HOVIV site.\"\n\n[10] \"We predict that at 750 K the apparent activation energy is 123  5 kJ/mol and the rate constant is about 0.26 s-1, in close agreement with experiments.\"\n\n[11] \"The first hydrogen abstraction occurs exclusively on OVV sites, while the second hydrogen abstraction can also occur on VOSi bridging oxygen sites.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts, particularly supported vanadium oxides, significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations in oxidative dehydrogenation (ODH) processes.\n\nComposition and Structure:\n1. The catalysts consist of V2O5 supported on SiO2, with monomeric OV(O)3 surface species, along with dimeric and oligomeric V2O5 species at low V2O5 loadings.\n2. The vanadium surface coverage is crucial, with optimal activity observed below 3 V/nm2, before V2O5 crystallites start to form.\n3. The active sites are modeled as vanadyl-substituted silsesquioxane, representing monomeric vanadium oxide species on silica.\n\nReaction Mechanism:\n1. The initial step involves hydrogen abstraction by the vanadyl (OVV) oxygen atom, forming a diradical intermediate (C3H7•·HOVIV(O)3).\n2. The first hydrogen abstraction occurs exclusively on OVV sites, while the second can also happen on VOSi bridging oxygen sites.\n3. Desorption of propyl radicals leads to a stationary concentration in the gas phase and leaves reduced HOVIV sites on the surface.\n4. Fast reoxidation maintains a low concentration of reduced sites compared to OVV sites.\n\nReaction Conditions and Efficiency:\n1. Temperature significantly affects the reaction kinetics. At 750 K, the apparent activation energy is predicted to be 123 ± 5 kJ/mol, with a rate constant of about 0.26 s-1.\n2. For V2O5/SiO2 catalysts with vanadium surface coverages below 3 V/nm2, turnover frequencies (TOF) of around 0.01 s-1 at 748 K and 0.3 × 10-3 s-1 at 623 K have been observed for propene formation.\n\nIndustrial Significance:\n1. The ability to fine-tune catalyst composition, structure, and reaction conditions allows for optimization of catalyst performance.\n2. Understanding the reaction mechanism and kinetics helps in designing more efficient catalysts for industrial applications.\n3. The comparison between supported monomers and bulk V2O5 surfaces suggests potential for improving catalyst activity, although accessibility of active sites in three-dimensional particles may limit apparent turnover frequencies.\n\nThese factors contribute to the industrial importance of supported vanadium oxide catalysts in alkane transformation processes, particularly in the production of valuable chemical intermediates from alkanes.", "Doi": "10.1021/jp071409e"},
{"Quotes": "[1] \"Several different compounds, particularly silica supported metal oxides [1,2] were used in the process. Recently it has been mentioned that ZSM-5 [3] and ZSM-5 combined with molybdenum [4,5] or tungsten [6] were investigated. Silica supported V2O5 and MoO3 were used most frequently. They are considered to be the most effective in the production of formaldehyde [7,8].\"\n\n[2] \"Fe-ZSM-5 was used as a catalyst for the room temperature methane oxidation by N2O [1317]. The activation of methane over Fe-ZSM-5 is believed to be similar to methane activation in MMO thus to employ a redox mechanism.\"\n\n[3] \"The reaction was carried out at the atmospheric pressure at 350650 8C. ZSM-5 molecular sieves with the iron and silicon tetrahedral framework were used as catalysts. The Si/Fe molar ratio was 45, 34 and 22. Fe-ZSM-5 was applied both in the sodium and hydrogen form.\"\n\n[4] \"Higher values of methane conversion were acquired when Fe-HZSM-5 (31.51% for Si/Fe = 22) was used. Fe-NaZSM-5 resulted in procuring higher values of the selectivity of the oxidation of methane to methanol (74% for Si/Fe = 45).\"\n\n[5] \"It was found that increasing the temperature as well as extending the contact time increases the conversion of methane to methanol and at the same time lowers the value of the selectivity of the oxidation of methane to methanol.\"\n\n[6] \"Increasing the iron content resulted in the rise of methane conversion. The selectivity of the oxidation of methane to oxygenates displayed a slight grow along with the drop of the iron content, while the selectivity of methane oxidation to carbon dioxide was falling.\"", "English": "The composition, structure, and reaction conditions of metal-based catalysts significantly influence the mechanisms and efficiency of alkane transformations:\n\n1. Catalyst composition: Various metal-based catalysts are used, including silica-supported metal oxides (V2O5, MoO3), metal-exchanged zeolites (Fe-ZSM-5), and ZSM-5 combined with molybdenum or tungsten. Silica-supported V2O5 and MoO3 are considered most effective for formaldehyde production.\n\n2. Catalyst structure: ZSM-5 molecular sieves with iron and silicon in the tetrahedral framework are used, with varying Si/Fe molar ratios (45, 34, 22). The catalyst can be in sodium (Fe-NaZSM-5) or hydrogen (Fe-HZSM-5) form, affecting its performance.\n\n3. Metal content: For Fe-ZSM-5 catalysts, increasing iron content (lower Si/Fe ratio) leads to higher methane conversion but lower selectivity to oxygenates and higher selectivity to carbon dioxide.\n\n4. Reaction conditions: \n   - Temperature: Reactions are typically carried out at 350-650°C. Higher temperatures increase methane conversion but decrease selectivity to methanol.\n   - Pressure: Atmospheric pressure is used.\n   - Contact time: Longer contact times increase methane conversion but decrease selectivity to methanol.\n\n5. Reaction mechanism: The activation of methane over Fe-ZSM-5 is believed to follow a redox mechanism similar to methane monooxygenase (MMO) in biological systems.\n\nThese factors contribute to industrial significance by allowing control over product distribution and reaction efficiency. For example, Fe-HZSM-5 shows higher methane conversion (31.51% for Si/Fe = 22), while Fe-NaZSM-5 demonstrates higher selectivity for methane to methanol oxidation (74% for Si/Fe = 45). The ability to fine-tune catalyst properties and reaction conditions enables the optimization of desired product yields and selectivities in processes such as oxidative dehydrogenation and zeolite-catalyzed reactions.", "Doi": "10.1016/j.apcata.2004.09.005"},
